</td></tr>

</tbody></table><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1220493105">html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota tr{background:transparent!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota img{background:white}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota tr{background:transparent!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota img{background:white}}</style>
<p>The <b>peach</b> (<i>Prunus persica</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Deciduous" title="Deciduous">deciduous</a> <a href="/wiki/Tree" title="Tree">tree</a> first <a href="/wiki/Domesticated" class="mw-redirect" title="Domesticated">domesticated</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">cultivated</a> in <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang province</a> of <a href="/wiki/East_China" title="East China">Eastern China</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-YangZheng20142_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YangZheng20142-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> It bears edible juicy <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruits</a> with various characteristics, most called <b>peaches</b> and others (the glossy-skinned, non-fuzzy varieties), <b>nectarines</b>.
</p><p>The specific name <i>persica</i> refers to its widespread cultivation in <a href="/wiki/Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Persia">Persia</a> (modern-day Iran), from where it was transplanted to Europe and in the 16th century to the Americas. It belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Prunus" title="Prunus">Prunus</a></i>, which includes the <a href="/wiki/Cherry" title="Cherry">cherry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apricot" title="Apricot">apricot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Almond" title="Almond">almond</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plum" title="Plum">plum</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Rosaceae" title="Rosaceae">rose family</a>. The peach is classified with the almond in the <a href="/wiki/Subgenus" title="Subgenus">subgenus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Amygdalus" class="mw-redirect" title="Amygdalus">Amygdalus</a></i>, distinguished from the other subgenera by the corrugated seed shell (<a href="/wiki/Endocarp" class="mw-redirect" title="Endocarp">endocarp</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Due to their close relatedness, the kernel of a peach stone tastes remarkably similar to almond, and peach stones are often used to make a cheap version of <a href="/wiki/Marzipan" title="Marzipan">marzipan</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Persipan" title="Persipan">persipan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Peaches and <a class="mw-selflink-fragment" href="#Nectarines">nectarines</a> are the same species, though they are regarded commercially as different fruits. The skin of nectarines lacks the fuzz (fruit-skin <a href="/wiki/Trichome" title="Trichome">trichomes</a>) that peach skin has; a mutation in a single <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">gene</a> (<i>MYB25</i>) is thought to be responsible for the difference between the two.<sup id="cite_ref-osu_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osu-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2018, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> produced 62% of the world total of peaches and nectarines. <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, all situated in the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Basin" title="Mediterranean Basin">Mediterranean region</a>, are prominent producers of peaches.<sup id="cite_ref-fao2018_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fao2018-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Amygdalus_persica2.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Amygdalus_persica2.jpg/220px-Amygdalus_persica2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Amygdalus_persica2.jpg/330px-Amygdalus_persica2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Amygdalus_persica2.jpg/440px-Amygdalus_persica2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="450" /></a><figcaption>Peach flowers</figcaption></figure>
<p><i>Prunus persica</i> trees grow up to 7&#160;m (23&#160;ft) tall and wide, but when pruned properly, they are usually 3–4&#160;m (10–13&#160;ft) tall and wide.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Leaf" title="Leaf">leaves</a> are <a href="/wiki/Lanceolate" class="mw-redirect" title="Lanceolate">lanceolate</a>, <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1154941027">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);clip-path:polygon(0px 0px,0px 0px,0px 0px);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style>7–16&#160;cm (3–<span class="frac">6<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span>&#160;in) long, <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1154941027">2–3&#160;cm (<span class="frac"><span class="num">3</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span>–<span class="frac">1<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span>&#160;in) broad, and <a href="/wiki/Pinnate" class="mw-redirect" title="Pinnate">pinnately</a> veined. The <a href="/wiki/Flower" title="Flower">flowers</a> are produced in early spring before the leaves; they are solitary or paired, 2.5–3&#160;cm diameter, pink, with five petals. The <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a> has yellow or whitish flesh, a delicate aroma, and a skin that is either velvety (peaches) or smooth (nectarines) in different <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivars</a>. The flesh is very delicate and easily bruised in some cultivars, but is fairly firm in some commercial varieties, especially when green. The single, large seed is red-brown, oval shaped, around 1.3–2&#160;cm long, and surrounded by a wood-like husk. Peaches, along with cherries, plums, and apricots, are stone fruits (<a href="/wiki/Drupe" title="Drupe">drupes</a>). The various <a href="/wiki/Heirloom_varieties" class="mw-redirect" title="Heirloom varieties">heirloom varieties</a> including the 'Indian Peach', or 'Indian Blood Peach', which ripens in the latter part of the summer, and can have color ranging from red and white, to purple.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Cultivated peaches are divided into <a href="/wiki/Drupe#Terminology" title="Drupe">clingstones and freestones</a>, depending on whether the flesh sticks to the <a href="/wiki/Pyrena" title="Pyrena">stone</a> or not; both can have either white or yellow flesh. Peaches with white flesh typically are very sweet with little <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acidity</a>, while yellow-fleshed peaches typically have an acidic tang coupled with sweetness, though this also varies greatly. Both colors often have some red on their skins. Low-acid, white-fleshed peaches are the most popular kinds in China, Japan, and neighbouring Asian countries, while Europeans and North Americans have historically favoured the acidic, yellow-fleshed <a href="/wiki/Cultivars" class="mw-redirect" title="Cultivars">cultivars</a>.
</p><p>Peach trees are relatively short-lived as compared with some other fruit trees. In some regions orchards are replanted after 8 to 10 years, while in others trees may produce satisfactorily for 20 to 25 years or more, depending upon their resistance to diseases, pests, and winter damage.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The scientific name <i>persica</i>, along with the word "peach" itself – and its cognates in many European languages – derives from an early European belief that peaches were native to <a href="/wiki/Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Persia">Persia</a> (modern-day Iran). The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Romans" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Romans">Ancient Romans</a> referred to the peach as <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">malum persicum</i></span> ("Persian apple"), later becoming French <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">pêche</i></span>, whence the English "peach".<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The scientific name, <i>Prunus persica</i>, literally means "Persian plum", as it is closely related to the <a href="/wiki/Plum" title="Plum">plum</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossil_record">Fossil record</span></h2>
<p>Fossil <a href="/wiki/Endocarp" class="mw-redirect" title="Endocarp">endocarps</a> with characteristics indistinguishable from those of modern peaches have been recovered from late <a href="/wiki/Pliocene" title="Pliocene">Pliocene</a> deposits in <a href="/wiki/Kunming" title="Kunming">Kunming</a>, dating to 2.6 million years ago. In the absence of evidence that the plants were in other ways identical to the modern peach, the name <i>Prunus kunmingensis</i> has been assigned to these fossils.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Dried_date,_peach,_apricot,_and_stones._From_Lahun,_Fayum,_Egypt._Late_Middle_Kingdom._The_Petrie_Museum_of_Egyptian_Archaeology,_London.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Dried_date%2C_peach%2C_apricot%2C_and_stones._From_Lahun%2C_Fayum%2C_Egypt._Late_Middle_Kingdom._The_Petrie_Museum_of_Egyptian_Archaeology%2C_London.jpg/220px-Dried_date%2C_peach%2C_apricot%2C_and_stones._From_Lahun%2C_Fayum%2C_Egypt._Late_Middle_Kingdom._The_Petrie_Museum_of_Egyptian_Archaeology%2C_London.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="159" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Dried_date%2C_peach%2C_apricot%2C_and_stones._From_Lahun%2C_Fayum%2C_Egypt._Late_Middle_Kingdom._The_Petrie_Museum_of_Egyptian_Archaeology%2C_London.jpg/330px-Dried_date%2C_peach%2C_apricot%2C_and_stones._From_Lahun%2C_Fayum%2C_Egypt._Late_Middle_Kingdom._The_Petrie_Museum_of_Egyptian_Archaeology%2C_London.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Dried_date%2C_peach%2C_apricot%2C_and_stones._From_Lahun%2C_Fayum%2C_Egypt._Late_Middle_Kingdom._The_Petrie_Museum_of_Egyptian_Archaeology%2C_London.jpg/440px-Dried_date%2C_peach%2C_apricot%2C_and_stones._From_Lahun%2C_Fayum%2C_Egypt._Late_Middle_Kingdom._The_Petrie_Museum_of_Egyptian_Archaeology%2C_London.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4471" data-file-height="3235" /></a><figcaption>Dried date, peach, apricot, and stones from Lahun, Fayum, Egypt, Late Middle Kingdom, <a href="/wiki/Petrie_Museum_of_Egyptian_Archaeology" title="Petrie Museum of Egyptian Archaeology">Petrie Museum of Egyptian Archaeology</a>, London</figcaption></figure>
<p>Although its botanical name <i>Prunus persica</i> refers to Persia, genetic studies suggest peaches originated in China,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> where they have been <a href="/wiki/Peach_production_in_China" title="Peach production in China">cultivated since the Neolithic period</a>. Until recently, cultivation was believed to have started around 2000 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vau09-82_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vau09-82-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> More recent evidence indicates that domestication occurred as early as 6000 BC in <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang_Province" class="mw-redirect" title="Zhejiang Province">Zhejiang Province</a> of China. The oldest archaeological peach stones are from the <a href="/wiki/Kuahuqiao" class="mw-redirect" title="Kuahuqiao">Kuahuqiao</a> site near <a href="/wiki/Hangzhou" title="Hangzhou">Hangzhou</a>. Archaeologists point to the <a href="/wiki/Yangtze_River_Valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Yangtze River Valley">Yangtze River Valley</a> as the place where the early selection for favorable peach varieties probably took place.<sup id="cite_ref-YangZheng20142_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YangZheng20142-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Peaches were mentioned in Chinese writings and literature beginning from the early first millennium BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Layne_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Layne-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A domesticated peach appeared very early in Japan, in 4700–4400 BC, during the <a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dmon_period" title="Jōmon period">Jōmon period</a>. It was already similar to modern cultivated forms, where the peach stones are significantly larger and more compressed than earlier stones. This domesticated type of peach was brought into Japan from China. Nevertheless, in China itself, this variety is currently attested only at a later date around 3300 to 2300 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-YangZheng2014_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YangZheng2014-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In India, the peach first appeared by about 1700 BC, during the <a href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Indus Valley civilisation">Harappan period</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is also found elsewhere in <a href="/wiki/West_Asia" title="West Asia">West Asia</a> in ancient times.<sup id="cite_ref-Ensminger_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Peach cultivation reached Greece by 300 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-vau09-82_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vau09-82-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Alexander the Great is sometimes said to have introduced them into Greece after conquering Persia,<sup id="cite_ref-Ensminger_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> but no historical evidence for this claim has been found.<sup id="cite_ref-Davidson_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davidson-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Peaches were, however, well known to the Romans in the first century AD;<sup id="cite_ref-vau09-82_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vau09-82-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> the oldest known artistic representations of the fruit are in two fragments of wall paintings, dated to the first century AD, in <a href="/wiki/Herculaneum" title="Herculaneum">Herculaneum</a>, preserved due to the <a href="/wiki/Vesuvius" class="mw-redirect" title="Vesuvius">Vesuvius</a> eruption of 79 AD, and now held in the National Archaeological Museum in Naples.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Archaeological finds show that peaches were cultivated widely in Roman northwestern Continental Europe, but production collapsed around the sixth century; some revival of production followed with the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Renaissance" title="Carolingian Renaissance">Carolingian Renaissance</a> of the ninth century.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An article on peach tree cultivation in Spain is brought down in <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-%27Awwam" title="Ibn al-&#39;Awwam">Ibn al-'Awwam</a>'s 12th-century agricultural work, <i>Book on Agriculture</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The peach was brought to the Americas by Spanish explorers in the 16th century, and eventually made it to England and France in the 17th century, where it was a prized and expensive treat. <a href="/wiki/Horticulturist" class="mw-redirect" title="Horticulturist">Horticulturist</a> George Minifie supposedly brought the first peaches from England to its North American colonies in the early 17th century, planting them at his estate of Buckland in Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Although Thomas Jefferson had peach trees at Monticello, American farmers did not begin commercial production until the 19th century in Maryland, Delaware, Georgia, South Carolina, and finally Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Shanghai honey nectar peach was a key component of both the food culture and agrarian economy the area where the modern megacity of <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a> stands. Peaches were the cornerstone of early Shanghai's garden culture. As modernization and westernization swept through the city the Shanghai honey nectar peach nearly disappeared completely. Much of modern Shanghai is built over these gardens and peach orchards.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 2010, an international consortium, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Peach_Genome_Initiative&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="International Peach Genome Initiative (page does not exist)">International Peach Genome Initiative</a>, which includes researchers from the United States, Italy, Chile, Spain, and France, announced they had <a href="/wiki/Sequencing" title="Sequencing">sequenced</a> the peach tree genome (doubled haploid Lovell). Recently, it published the peach genome sequence and related analyses. The sequence is composed of 227 million nucleotides arranged in eight pseudomolecules representing the eight peach chromosomes (2n = 16). In addition, 27,852 protein-coding genes and 28,689 protein-coding transcripts were predicted.
</p><p>Particular emphasis in this study is reserved for the analysis of the genetic diversity in peach germplasm and how it was shaped by human activities such as domestication and breeding. Major historical bottlenecks were found, one related to the putative original domestication that is supposed to have taken place in China about 4,000–5,000 years ago, the second is related to the western germplasm and is due to the early dissemination of the peach in Europe from China and the more recent breeding activities in the United States and Europe. These bottlenecks highlighted the substantial reduction of genetic diversity associated with domestication and breeding activities.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peaches_in_the_Americas">Peaches in the Americas</span></h3>
<p>Peaches were introduced into the Americas in the 16th century by the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish</a>. By 1580, peaches were being grown in Latin America and were cultivated by the remnants of the <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca Empire</a> in Argentina.<sup id="cite_ref-Capparelli_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Capparelli-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>  
</p><p>In the United States the peach was soon adopted as a crop by <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Native Americans of the United States">American Indians</a>. The peach also became naturalized and abundant as a wild species in the eastern U.S.. Peaches were being grown in Virginia as early as 1629. Peaches grown by Indians in Virginia were said to have been "of greater variety and finer sorts" than those of the English colonists. Also in 1629, peaches were listed as a crop in <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>. <a href="/wiki/William_Penn" title="William Penn">William Penn</a> noted the existence of wild peaches in <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> in 1683.<sup id="cite_ref-Jett_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jett-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Peaches_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peaches-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>  
</p><p>Peach plantations became an objective of American military campaigns against the Indians. In 1779, the <a href="/wiki/Sullivan_Expedition" title="Sullivan Expedition">Sullivan Expedition</a> destroyed the livelihood of many of the <a href="/wiki/Iroquois" title="Iroquois">Iroquois</a> people of <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>. Among the crops destroyed were plantations of peach trees.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In 1864, <a href="/wiki/Kit_Carson" title="Kit Carson">Kit Carson</a> led a successful U.S. army expedition to <a href="/wiki/Canyon_de_Chelly_National_Monument" title="Canyon de Chelly National Monument">Canyon de Chelly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a> to destroy the livelihood of the <a href="/wiki/Navajo" title="Navajo">Navajo</a>. Carson destroyed thousands of peach trees. A soldier said they were the "best peach trees I have ever seen in the country, every one of them bearing fruit.” <sup id="cite_ref-Sumiak_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sumiak-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultivation">Cultivation</span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Bee_pollinating_peach_flower.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Bee_pollinating_peach_flower.jpg/220px-Bee_pollinating_peach_flower.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Bee_pollinating_peach_flower.jpg/330px-Bee_pollinating_peach_flower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Bee_pollinating_peach_flower.jpg/440px-Bee_pollinating_peach_flower.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1067" /></a><figcaption>A peach flower with a <a href="/wiki/Bee" title="Bee">bee</a> pollinating it</figcaption></figure>
<p>Peaches grow in a fairly limited range in dry, continental or temperate climates, since the trees have a <a href="/wiki/Chilling_requirement" title="Chilling requirement">chilling requirement</a> that tropical or subtropical areas generally do not satisfy except at high altitudes (for example in certain areas of <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, India, and <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>). Most cultivars require 500 hours of chilling around 0 to 10&#160;°C (32 to 50&#160;°F). During the chilling period, key chemical reactions occur, but the plant appears dormant. Once the chilling period is fulfilled, the plant enters a second type of dormancy, the <a href="/wiki/Dormancy#Trees" title="Dormancy">quiescence</a> period. During quiescence, buds break and grow when sufficient warm weather favorable to growth is accumulated.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The trees themselves can usually tolerate temperatures to around −26 to −30&#160;°C (−15 to −22&#160;°F), although the following season's flower buds are usually killed at these temperatures, preventing a crop that summer. Flower bud death begins to occur between −15 and −25&#160;°C (5 and −13&#160;°F), depending on the <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivar</a> and on the timing of the cold, with the buds becoming less cold tolerant in late winter.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another climate constraint is spring frost. The trees flower fairly early (in March in Western Europe), and the blossom is damaged or killed if temperatures drop below about −4&#160;°C (25&#160;°F). If the flowers are not fully open, though, they can tolerate a few degrees colder.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Climates with significant winter rainfall at temperatures below 16&#160;°C (61&#160;°F) are also unsuitable for peach cultivation, as the rain promotes <a href="/wiki/Leaf_curl" title="Leaf curl">peach leaf curl</a>, which is the most serious fungal disease for peaches. In practice, fungicides are extensively used for peach cultivation in such climates, with more than 1% of European peaches exceeding legal pesticide limits in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Finally, summer heat is required to mature the crop, with mean temperatures of the hottest month between 20 and 30&#160;°C (68 and 86&#160;°F).
</p><p>Typical peach cultivars begin bearing fruit in their third year. Their lifespan in the U.S. varies by region; the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California_at_Davis" class="mw-redirect" title="University of California at Davis">University of California at Davis</a> gives a lifespan of about 15 years<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Maine" title="University of Maine">University of Maine</a> gives a lifespan of 7 years there.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultivars">Cultivars</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:White_peach_and_cross_section_edit.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/White_peach_and_cross_section_edit.jpg/290px-White_peach_and_cross_section_edit.jpg" decoding="async" width="290" height="117" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/White_peach_and_cross_section_edit.jpg/435px-White_peach_and_cross_section_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/White_peach_and_cross_section_edit.jpg/580px-White_peach_and_cross_section_edit.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2369" data-file-height="955" /></a><figcaption>White peach of the clingstone variety</figcaption></figure>
<p>Hundreds of peach and nectarine cultivars are known. These are classified into two categories—freestones and clingstones. Freestones are those whose flesh separates readily from the pit. Clingstones are those whose flesh clings tightly to the pit. Some cultivars are partially freestone and clingstone, so are called semifree. Freestone types are preferred for eating fresh, while clingstone types are for canning. The fruit flesh may be creamy white to deep yellow, to dark red; the hue and shade of the color depend on the cultivar.<sup id="cite_ref-uri2000_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uri2000-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Peach breeding has favored cultivars with more firmness, more red color, and shorter fuzz on the fruit surface. These characteristics ease shipping and supermarket sales by improving eye appeal. This selection process has not necessarily led to increased flavor, though. Peaches have a short shelf life, so commercial growers typically plant a mix of different cultivars to have fruit to ship all season long.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Different countries have different cultivars. In the United Kingdom, for example, these cultivars have gained the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Horticultural_Society" title="Royal Horticultural Society">Royal Horticultural Society</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Award_of_Garden_Merit" title="Award of Garden Merit">Award of Garden Merit</a>:
</p>
<ul><li>'Duke of York'<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>'Peregrine'<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>'Rochester'<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>'Lord Napier' (nectarine)<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>For China specifically see <a href="/wiki/Peach_production_in_China#Cultivars" title="Peach production in China">Peach production in China §&#160;Cultivars</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nectarines">Nectarines</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:White_nectarine_and_cross_section02_edit.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/White_nectarine_and_cross_section02_edit.jpg/220px-White_nectarine_and_cross_section02_edit.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="117" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/White_nectarine_and_cross_section02_edit.jpg/330px-White_nectarine_and_cross_section02_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/White_nectarine_and_cross_section02_edit.jpg/440px-White_nectarine_and_cross_section02_edit.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1891" data-file-height="1006" /></a><figcaption>White nectarines, whole and cut open</figcaption></figure>
<p>The variety <i>P. persica</i> var. <i>nucipersica</i> (or var. <i>nectarina</i>) – these are commonly called <i>nectarines</i> – has a smooth skin. It is on occasion referred to as a "shaved peach" or "fuzzless peach", due to its lack of fuzz or short hairs. Though fuzzy peaches and nectarines are regarded commercially as different fruits, with nectarines often erroneously believed to be a crossbreed between peaches and <a href="/wiki/Plum" title="Plum">plums</a>, or a "peach with a plum skin", nectarines belong to the same species as peaches. Several genetic studies have concluded nectarines are produced due to a <a href="/wiki/Recessive_allele" class="mw-redirect" title="Recessive allele">recessive allele</a>, whereas a fuzzy peach skin is <a href="/wiki/Dominant_gene" class="mw-redirect" title="Dominant gene">dominant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-osu_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osu-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Nectarines have arisen many times from peach trees, often as <a href="/wiki/Bud_sport" class="mw-redirect" title="Bud sport">bud sports</a>.
</p><p>As with peaches, nectarines can be white or yellow, and clingstone or freestone. On average, nectarines are slightly smaller and sweeter than peaches, but with much overlap.<sup id="cite_ref-osu_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osu-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The lack of skin fuzz can make nectarine skins appear more reddish than those of peaches, contributing to the fruit's plum-like appearance. The lack of down on nectarines' skin also means their skin is more easily bruised than peaches.
</p><p>The history of the nectarine is unclear; the first recorded mention in English is from 1616,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> but they had probably been grown much earlier within the native range of the peach in central and eastern Asia. Although one source states that nectarines were introduced into the United States by <a href="/wiki/David_Fairchild" title="David Fairchild">David Fairchild</a> of the Department of Agriculture in 1906,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> a number of colonial-era newspaper articles make reference to nectarines being grown in the United States prior to the Revolutionary War. The 28 March 1768 edition of the <i>New York Gazette</i> (p.&#160;3), for example, mentions a farm in Jamaica, Long Island, New York, where nectarines were grown.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peacherines">Peacherines</span></h3>
<p>Peacherines are claimed to be a cross between a peach and a nectarine, but as they are the same species cannot be a true cross (hybrid); they are marketed in Australia and New Zealand. The fruit is intermediate in appearance, though, between a peach and a nectarine, large and brightly colored like a red peach. The flesh of the fruit is usually yellow, but white varieties also exist. The <a href="/w/index.php?title=Koanga_Institute&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Koanga Institute (page does not exist)">Koanga Institute</a> lists varieties that ripen in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a> in February and March.<sup id="cite_ref-Koanga_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koanga-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1909, <i>Pacific Monthly</i> mentioned peacherines in a news bulletin for California. Louise Pound, in 1920, claimed the term peacherine is an example of language stunt.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flat_peaches">Flat peaches</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Flat_peach" title="Flat peach">Flat peach</a></div>
<p>Flat peaches, or <i>pan-tao</i>, have a flattened shape, in contrast to ordinary near-spherical peaches.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Planting">Planting</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Nectarine_Fruit_Development.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Nectarine_Fruit_Development.jpg/220px-Nectarine_Fruit_Development.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="224" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Nectarine_Fruit_Development.jpg/330px-Nectarine_Fruit_Development.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Nectarine_Fruit_Development.jpg/440px-Nectarine_Fruit_Development.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2440" data-file-height="2480" /></a><figcaption>The developmental sequence of a nectarine over a <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1154941027"><span class="frac">7<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span>-month period, from bud formation in early winter to fruit <a href="/wiki/Ripening" title="Ripening">ripening</a> in midsummer</figcaption></figure>
<p>Most peach trees sold by nurseries are cultivars <a href="/wiki/Shield_budding" title="Shield budding">budded</a> or <a href="/wiki/Grafted" class="mw-redirect" title="Grafted">grafted</a> onto a suitable <a href="/wiki/Rootstock" title="Rootstock">rootstock</a>. Common rootstocks are 'Lovell Peach', 'Nemaguard Peach', <i>Prunus besseyi</i>, and 'Citation'.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The rootstock provides hardiness and budding is done to improve predictability of the fruit quality.
</p><p>Peach trees need full sun, and a layout that allows good natural air flow to assist the thermal environment for the tree. Peaches are planted in early winter. During the growth season, they need a regular and reliable supply of water, with higher amounts just before harvest.<sup id="cite_ref-peachorchard_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-peachorchard-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Peaches need <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>-rich fertilizers more than other fruit trees. Without regular fertilizer supply, peach tree leaves start turning yellow or exhibit stunted growth. <a href="/wiki/Blood_meal" title="Blood meal">Blood meal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bone_meal" title="Bone meal">bone meal</a>, and calcium ammonium nitrate are suitable fertilizers.
</p><p>The flowers on a peach tree are typically thinned out because if the full number of peaches mature on a branch, they are undersized and lack flavor. Fruits are thinned midway in the season by commercial growers. Fresh peaches are easily bruised, so do not store well. They are most flavorful when they ripen on the tree and are eaten the day of harvest.<sup id="cite_ref-peachorchard_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-peachorchard-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The peach tree can be grown in an <a href="/wiki/Espalier" title="Espalier">espalier</a> shape. The Baldassari palmette is a design created around 1950 used primarily for training peaches. In <a href="/wiki/Walled_garden" title="Walled garden">walled gardens</a> constructed from stone or brick, which absorb and retain solar heat and then slowly release it, raising the temperature against the wall, peaches can be grown as espaliers against south-facing walls as far north as southeast Great Britain and southern Ireland.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Insects">Insects</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">April 2018</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The first <a href="/wiki/Pest_(organism)" title="Pest (organism)">pest</a> to attack the tree early in the year when other food is scarce is the earwig (<i><a href="/wiki/Forficula_auricularia" title="Forficula auricularia">Forficula auricularia</a></i>) which feeds on <a href="/wiki/Blossom" title="Blossom">blossoms</a> and young leaves at night, preventing fruiting and weakening newly planted trees. The pattern of damage is distinct from that of <a href="/wiki/Caterpillars" class="mw-redirect" title="Caterpillars">caterpillars</a> later in the year, as earwigs characteristically remove semicircles of petal and leaf tissue from the tips, rather than internally. <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greaseband&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Greaseband (page does not exist)">Greasebands</a> applied just before blossom are effective.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (April 2018)">failed verification</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The larvae of such <a href="/wiki/Moth" title="Moth">moth</a> species as the peachtree borer (<i><a href="/wiki/Synanthedon_exitiosa" title="Synanthedon exitiosa">Synanthedon exitiosa</a></i>), the yellow peach moth (<i><a href="/wiki/Conogethes_punctiferalis" title="Conogethes punctiferalis">Conogethes punctiferalis</a></i>), the well-marked cutworm (<i><a href="/wiki/Abagrotis_orbis" title="Abagrotis orbis">Abagrotis orbis</a></i>), <i><a href="/wiki/Lyonetia_prunifoliella" title="Lyonetia prunifoliella">Lyonetia prunifoliella</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Phyllonorycter_hostis" title="Phyllonorycter hostis">Phyllonorycter hostis</a></i>, the fruit tree borer (<i><a href="/wiki/Maroga_melanostigma" title="Maroga melanostigma">Maroga melanostigma</a></i>), <i><a href="/wiki/Parornix_anguliferella" title="Parornix anguliferella">Parornix anguliferella</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Parornix_finitimella" title="Parornix finitimella">Parornix finitimella</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Caloptilia_zachrysa" title="Caloptilia zachrysa">Caloptilia zachrysa</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Phyllonorycter_crataegella" title="Phyllonorycter crataegella">Phyllonorycter crataegella</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Trifurcula_sinica" title="Trifurcula sinica">Trifurcula sinica</a></i>, Suzuki's promolactis moth (<i><a href="/wiki/Promalactis_suzukiella" title="Promalactis suzukiella">Promalactis suzukiella</a></i>), the white-spotted <a href="/wiki/Tussock_moth" class="mw-redirect" title="Tussock moth">tussock moth</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Orgyia_thyellina" title="Orgyia thyellina">Orgyia thyellina</a></i>), the apple leafroller (<i><a href="/wiki/Archips_termias" title="Archips termias">Archips termias</a></i>), the catapult moth (<i><a href="/wiki/Serrodes_partita" title="Serrodes partita">Serrodes partita</a></i>), the wood groundling (<i><a href="/wiki/Parachronistis_albiceps" title="Parachronistis albiceps">Parachronistis albiceps</a></i>) or the omnivorous leafroller (<i><a href="/wiki/Platynota_stultana" title="Platynota stultana">Platynota stultana</a></i>) are reported to feed on <i>P. persica</i>. The flatid planthopper (<i><a href="/wiki/Metcalfa_pruinosa" title="Metcalfa pruinosa">Metcalfa pruinosa</a></i>) causes damage to fruit trees.
</p><p>The tree is also a host plant for such species as the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_beetle" title="Japanese beetle">Japanese beetle</a> (<i>Popillia japonica</i>), the unmonsuzume (<i><a href="/wiki/Callambulyx_tatarinovii" title="Callambulyx tatarinovii">Callambulyx tatarinovii</a></i>), the promethea silkmoth (<i><a href="/wiki/Callosamia_promethea" title="Callosamia promethea">Callosamia promethea</a></i>), the orange oakleaf (<i><a href="/wiki/Kallima_inachus" title="Kallima inachus">Kallima inachus</a></i>), <i><a href="/wiki/Langia_zenzeroides" title="Langia zenzeroides">Langia zenzeroides</a></i>, the speckled emperor (<i><a href="/wiki/Gynanisa_maja" title="Gynanisa maja">Gynanisa maja</a></i>) or the brown playboy (<i><a href="/wiki/Deudorix_antalus" title="Deudorix antalus">Deudorix antalus</a></i>). The European red mite (<i><a href="/wiki/Panonychus_ulmi" title="Panonychus ulmi">Panonychus ulmi</a></i>) or the yellow mite (<i><a href="/wiki/Lorryia_formosa" title="Lorryia formosa">Lorryia formosa</a></i>) are also found on the peach tree.
</p><p>It is a good <a href="/wiki/Pollen_source" class="mw-redirect" title="Pollen source">pollen source</a> for <a href="/wiki/Honey_bees" class="mw-redirect" title="Honey bees">honey bees</a> and a <a href="/wiki/List_of_honeydew_sources" title="List of honeydew sources">honeydew source</a> for aphids.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diseases">Diseases</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_peach_and_nectarine_diseases" title="List of peach and nectarine diseases">List of peach and nectarine diseases</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Preparing_peaches_to_be_canned.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Preparing_peaches_to_be_canned.jpg/220px-Preparing_peaches_to_be_canned.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Preparing_peaches_to_be_canned.jpg/330px-Preparing_peaches_to_be_canned.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Preparing_peaches_to_be_canned.jpg/440px-Preparing_peaches_to_be_canned.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1365" /></a><figcaption>Because peaches are highly perishable, farmers around the world often will preserve peaches through <a href="/wiki/Canning" title="Canning">canning</a> or production of preserves. This mother-daughter team in <a href="/wiki/Lesotho" title="Lesotho">Lesotho</a> is preparing peaches near their farm.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Peach trees are prone to a disease called <a href="/wiki/Leaf_curl" title="Leaf curl">leaf curl</a>, which usually does not directly affect the fruit, but does reduce the crop yield by partially defoliating the tree. Several fungicides can be used to combat the disease, including <a href="/wiki/Bordeaux_mixture" title="Bordeaux mixture">Bordeaux mixture</a> and other copper-based products (the University of California considers these organic treatments), <a href="/wiki/Ziram" class="mw-redirect" title="Ziram">ziram</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlorothalonil" title="Chlorothalonil">chlorothalonil</a>, and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dodine&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Dodine (page does not exist)">dodine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The fruit is susceptible to <a href="/wiki/Monilinia_fructicola" title="Monilinia fructicola">brown rot</a> or a dark reddish spot.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Storage">Storage</span></h3>
<p>Peaches and nectarines are best stored at temperatures of 0&#160;°C (32&#160;°F) and in high humidity.<sup id="cite_ref-uri2000_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uri2000-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> They are highly perishable, so are typically consumed or canned within two weeks of harvest.
</p><p>Peaches are <a href="/wiki/Climacteric_(botany)" title="Climacteric (botany)">climacteric</a><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> fruits and continue to ripen after being picked from the tree.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<p>In 2020, world production of peaches (combined with nectarines for reporting) was 24.6 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>, led by China with 61% of the world total (table).
</p><p>The U.S. state of <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a> is known as the "Peach State" due to its significant production of peaches as early as 1571,<sup id="cite_ref-georgia_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-georgia-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> with exports to other states occurring around 1858.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> In 2014, Georgia was third in US peach production behind <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-georgia_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-georgia-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The largest peach producing countries in <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a> are <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_significance">Cultural significance</span></h2>
<p>Peaches are not only a popular fruit, but also are symbolic in many cultural traditions, such as in art, paintings, and folk tales such as the <a href="/wiki/Peaches_of_Immortality" title="Peaches of Immortality">Peaches of Immortality</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Peaches_of_Immortality" title="Peaches of Immortality">Peaches of Immortality</a></div>
<p>Peach blossoms are highly prized in Chinese culture. The ancient Chinese believed the peach to possess more vitality than any other tree because their blossoms appear before leaves sprout. When early rulers of China visited their territories, they were preceded by sorcerers armed with peach rods to protect them from spectral evils. On New Year's Eve, local magistrates would cut peach wood branches and place them over their doors to protect against evil influences.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Peach wood was also used for the earliest known <a href="/wiki/Door_god" class="mw-redirect" title="Door god">door gods</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han</a>. Another author writes:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1211633275">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The Chinese also considered peach wood (<i>t'ao-fu</i>) protective against evil spirits, who held the peach in awe. In ancient China, peach-wood bows were used to shoot arrows in every direction in an effort to dispel evil. Peach-wood slips or carved pits served as amulets to protect a person's life, safety, and health.<sup id="cite_ref-China_1991,_Page_218_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-China_1991,_Page_218-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Peachwood seals or figurines guarded gates and doors, and, as one Han account recites, "the buildings in the capital are made tranquil and pure; everywhere a good state of affairs prevails".<sup id="cite_ref-China_1991,_Page_218_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-China_1991,_Page_218-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Writes the author, further:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1211633275"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Another aid in fighting evil spirits were peach-wood wands. The Li-chi (Han period) reported that the emperor went to the funeral of a minister escorted by a sorcerer carrying a peachwood wand to keep bad influences away. Since that time, peachwood wands have remained an important means of exorcism in China.<sup id="cite_ref-China_1991,_Page_218_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-China_1991,_Page_218-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote><p> Peach kernels (桃仁 <i>táo rén</i>) are a common ingredient used in <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">traditional Chinese medicine</a> to dispel blood <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stasis_(medicine)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Stasis (medicine) (page does not exist)">stasis</a>, counter inflammation, and reduce allergies.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In an orchard of flowering peach trees, <a href="/wiki/Liu_Bei" title="Liu Bei">Liu Bei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guan_Yu" title="Guan Yu">Guan Yu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Fei" title="Zhang Fei">Zhang Fei</a> took <a href="/wiki/Oath_of_the_Peach_Garden" title="Oath of the Peach Garden">an oath of brotherhood</a> in the opening chapter of the classic Chinese novel <i><a href="/wiki/Romance_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Romance of the Three Kingdoms">Romance of the Three Kingdoms</a></i>. Another peach orchard, in "<a href="/wiki/The_Peach_Blossom_Spring" title="The Peach Blossom Spring">The Peach Blossom Spring</a>" by poet <a href="/wiki/Tao_Yuanming" title="Tao Yuanming">Tao Yuanming</a>, is the setting of the favourite Chinese fable and a metaphor for utopias. A peach tree growing on a precipice was where the Taoist master <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Daoling" title="Zhang Daoling">Zhang Daoling</a> tested his disciples.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Old_Man_of_the_South_Pole" title="Old Man of the South Pole">Old Man of the South Pole</a>, one of the deities of the Chinese folk religion <i><a href="/wiki/Fu_Lu_Shou" class="mw-redirect" title="Fu Lu Shou">fulu shou</a></i>, is sometimes seen holding a large peach, representing long life and health.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The term "<a href="/wiki/Homosexuality_in_China#Terminology" title="Homosexuality in China">bitten peach</a>", first used by <a href="/wiki/Legalism_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Legalism (Chinese philosophy)">Legalist philosopher</a> <a href="/wiki/Han_Fei" title="Han Fei">Han Fei</a> in his work <i>Han Feizi</i>, became a byword for homosexuality. The book records the incident when courtier <a href="/wiki/Mizi_Xia" title="Mizi Xia">Mizi Xia</a> bit into an especially delicious peach and gave the remainder to his lover, <a href="/wiki/Duke_Ling_of_Wei" class="mw-redirect" title="Duke Ling of Wei">Duke Ling of Wei</a>, as a gift so that he could taste it, as well.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Korea">Korea</span></h3>
<p>In Korea, peaches have been cultivated from ancient times. According to <i><a href="/wiki/Samguk_Sagi" class="mw-redirect" title="Samguk Sagi">Samguk Sagi</a></i>, peach trees were planted during the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea" title="Three Kingdoms of Korea">Three Kingdoms of Korea</a> period, and <i><a href="/wiki/Sallim_gyeongje" title="Sallim gyeongje">Sallim gyeongje</a></i> also mentions cultivation skills of peach trees. The peach is seen as the fruit of happiness, riches, honours, and longevity. The rare peach with double seeds is seen as a favorable omen of a mild winter. It is one of the 10 immortal plants and animals, so peaches appear in many <i><a href="/wiki/Minhwa" title="Minhwa">minhwa</a></i> (folk paintings). Peaches and peach trees are believed to chase away spirits, so peaches are not placed on tables for <i><a href="/wiki/Jesa" title="Jesa">jesa</a></i> (ancestor veneration), unlike other fruits.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Japanese_Fairy_Book_-_Ozaki_-_247.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Japanese_Fairy_Book_-_Ozaki_-_247.png/220px-Japanese_Fairy_Book_-_Ozaki_-_247.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="211" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Japanese_Fairy_Book_-_Ozaki_-_247.png/330px-Japanese_Fairy_Book_-_Ozaki_-_247.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Japanese_Fairy_Book_-_Ozaki_-_247.png/440px-Japanese_Fairy_Book_-_Ozaki_-_247.png 2x" data-file-width="1401" data-file-height="1343" /></a><figcaption>Momotarō emerges from a peach.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The world's sweetest peach is grown in <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_(city)" title="Fukushima (city)">Fukushima</a>, Japan. The Guinness world record for the sweetest peach is currently held by a peach grown in Kanechika, Japan, with a sugar content of 22.2%. However, a fruit farm in rural Fukushima, Koji grew a much sweeter peach, with a Brix score of 32°. Degrees <a href="/wiki/Brix" title="Brix">Brix</a> measures the sugar content of the fruit, and is usually between 11 and 15 for a typical peach from a supermarket.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Momotar%C5%8D" title="Momotarō">Momotarō</a>, a folktale character, is named after the giant peach from which he was birthed.
</p><p>Two traditional Japanese words for the color <a href="/wiki/Pink" title="Pink">pink</a> correspond to blossoming trees: one for peach blossoms (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">momo-iro</i></span>), and one for cherry blossoms (<a href="/wiki/Cherry_blossom#Symbolism_in_Japan" title="Cherry blossom"><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">sakura-iro</i></span></a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vietnam">Vietnam</span></h3>
<p>A Vietnamese mythic history states that in the spring of 1789, after marching to Ngọc Hồi and then winning a great victory against invaders from the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a> of China, Emperor <a href="/wiki/Quang_Trung" title="Quang Trung">Quang Trung</a> ordered a messenger to gallop to <a href="/wiki/Ph%C3%BA_Xu%C3%A2n" title="Phú Xuân">Phú Xuân</a> citadel (now <a href="/wiki/Hu%E1%BA%BF" title="Huế">Huế</a>) and deliver a flowering peach branch to the Empress <a href="/wiki/L%C3%AA_Ng%E1%BB%8Dc_H%C3%A2n" title="Lê Ngọc Hân">Ngọc Hân</a>. This took place on the fifth day of the first lunar month, two days before the predicted end of the battle. The branch of peach flowers that was sent from the north to the centre of Vietnam was not only a message of victory from the Emperor to his consort, but also the start of a new spring of peace and happiness for all the Vietnamese people. In addition, since the land of Nhật Tân had freely given that very branch of peach flowers to the Emperor, it became the loyal garden of his dynasty.
</p><p>The protagonists of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Kieu" title="The Tale of Kieu">The Tale of Kieu</a></i> fell in love by a peach tree, and in <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, the blossoming peach flower is the signal of spring. Finally, peach <a href="/wiki/Bonsai" title="Bonsai">bonsai</a> trees are used as decoration during Vietnamese New Year (<a href="/wiki/T%E1%BA%BFt" title="Tết">Tết</a>) in northern Vietnam.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Pierre-Auguste_Renoir_-_P%C3%AAches.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Pierre-Auguste_Renoir_-_P%C3%AAches.jpg/220px-Pierre-Auguste_Renoir_-_P%C3%AAches.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="179" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Pierre-Auguste_Renoir_-_P%C3%AAches.jpg/330px-Pierre-Auguste_Renoir_-_P%C3%AAches.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Pierre-Auguste_Renoir_-_P%C3%AAches.jpg/440px-Pierre-Auguste_Renoir_-_P%C3%AAches.jpg 2x" data-file-width="550" data-file-height="448" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Pierre-Auguste_Renoir" title="Pierre-Auguste Renoir">Pierre-Auguste Renoir</a>, <i>A Still Life Painting of Peaches</i>, 1881–82</figcaption></figure>
<p>Many famous artists have painted with peach fruits placed in prominence. <a href="/wiki/Caravaggio" title="Caravaggio">Caravaggio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vicenzo_Campi" class="mw-redirect" title="Vicenzo Campi">Vicenzo Campi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Auguste_Renoir" title="Pierre-Auguste Renoir">Pierre-Auguste Renoir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Claude_Monet" title="Claude Monet">Claude Monet</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89douard_Manet" title="Édouard Manet">Édouard Manet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henri_Fantin-Latour" title="Henri Fantin-Latour">Henri Fantin-Latour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Severin_Roesen" title="Severin Roesen">Severin Roesen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Paul_Rubens" title="Peter Paul Rubens">Peter Paul Rubens</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Van_Gogh" class="mw-redirect" title="Van Gogh">Van Gogh</a> are among the many influential artists who painted peaches and peach trees in various settings.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-janick_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-janick-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Scholars suggest that many compositions are symbolic, some an effort to introduce realism.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> For example, Tresidder claims<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> the artists of Renaissance symbolically used peach to represent heart, and a leaf attached to the fruit as the symbol for tongue, thereby implying speaking truth from one's heart; a ripe peach was also a symbol to imply a ripe state of good health. Caravaggio's paintings introduce realism by painting peach leaves that are molted, discolored, or in some cases have wormholes – conditions common in modern peach cultivation.<sup id="cite_ref-janick_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-janick-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In literature, <a href="/wiki/Roald_Dahl" title="Roald Dahl">Roald Dahl</a> named his children's fantasy novel <i><a href="/wiki/James_and_the_Giant_Peach" title="James and the Giant Peach">James and the Giant Peach</a></i> because a peach is "prettier, bigger and squishier than a cherry."<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Peaches_in_baskets.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Peaches_in_baskets.jpg/180px-Peaches_in_baskets.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="130" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Peaches_in_baskets.jpg/270px-Peaches_in_baskets.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Peaches_in_baskets.jpg/360px-Peaches_in_baskets.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3535" data-file-height="2546" /></a><figcaption>Peaches at a roadside stand in South Carolina</figcaption></figure>
<p>South Carolina named the peach its <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._state_foods" title="List of U.S. state foods">official fruit</a> in 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The peach became the state fruit of <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>, nicknamed the "Peach State", in 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The peach went from feral trees utilized opportunistically to a tended commercial crop in the Southern United States in the 1850s, as the <a href="/wiki/Boll_weevil" title="Boll weevil">boll weevil</a> attacked regional cotton crops. When Georgia reached peak production in the 1920s, elaborate festivals celebrated the fruit. By 2017, Georgia's production represented 3–5% of the U.S. total.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Alabama named it the "state tree fruit" in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Delaware's <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._state_and_territory_flowers" title="List of U.S. state and territory flowers">state flower</a> has been the peach blossom since 1995,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> and peach pie became its official dessert in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition">Nutrition</span></h2>

</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data"><hr /><div class="wrap" style="padding:0.3em;line-height:1.2em;"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://fdc.nal.usda.gov/fdc-app.html#/food-details/169928/nutrients">Link to USDA Database entry</a></div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-below wrap" style="background:#e0e0e0;padding:0.3em;line-height:1.5em;font-weight:normal;"><sup>†</sup>Percentages estimated using <a href="/wiki/Reference_Daily_Intake#Daily_Values" title="Reference Daily Intake">US&#160;recommendations</a> for adults,<sup id="cite_ref-FDADailyValues_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FDADailyValues-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> except for potassium, which is estimated based on expert recommendation from <a href="/wiki/National_Academies_of_Sciences,_Engineering,_and_Medicine" title="National Academies of Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine">the National Academies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NationalAcademiesPotassium_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NationalAcademiesPotassium-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Raw peach flesh is 89% water, 10% <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, 1% <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>, and contains negligible <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a>. A medium-sized raw peach, weighing 100&#160;g (3.5&#160;oz), supplies 39 <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">calories</a>, and contains small amounts of <a href="/wiki/Essential_nutrients" class="mw-redirect" title="Essential nutrients">essential nutrients</a>, but none is a significant proportion of the <a href="/wiki/Daily_Value" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily Value">Daily Value</a> (DV, right table). A raw <a href="/wiki/Nectarine" class="mw-redirect" title="Nectarine">nectarine</a> has similar low content of nutrients.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Glycemic_load" title="Glycemic load">glycemic load</a> of an average peach (120 grams) is 5, similar to other low-sugar fruits.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One medium peach also contains 2% or more daily value of vitamins E and K, niacin, folate, iron, choline, potassium, magnesium, phosphorus, manganese, zinc and copper. Fresh peaches are a moderate source of antioxidants and vitamin C which is required for the building of connective tissue inside the human body.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phytochemicals">Phytochemicals</span></h2>
<p>Total <a href="/wiki/Polyphenols" class="mw-redirect" title="Polyphenols">polyphenols</a> in mg per 100&#160;g of fresh weight were 14–102 in white-flesh nectarines, 18–54 in yellow-flesh nectarines, 28–111 in white-flesh peaches, and 21–61&#160;mg per 100&#160;g in yellow-flesh peaches.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The major phenolic compounds identified in peach are <a href="/wiki/Chlorogenic_acid" title="Chlorogenic acid">chlorogenic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catechin" title="Catechin">catechins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epicatechins" class="mw-redirect" title="Epicatechins">epicatechins</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> with other compounds, identified by <a href="/wiki/High-performance_liquid_chromatography" title="High-performance liquid chromatography">HPLC</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Gallic_acid" title="Gallic acid">gallic acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ellagic_acid" title="Ellagic acid">ellagic acid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rutin" title="Rutin">Rutin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isoquercetin" title="Isoquercetin">isoquercetin</a> are the primary flavonols found in clingstone peaches.<sup id="cite_ref-Chang_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chang-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Red-fleshed peaches are rich in <a href="/wiki/Anthocyanin" title="Anthocyanin">anthocyanins</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> particularly <a href="/wiki/Cyanidin" title="Cyanidin">cyanidin</a> <a href="/wiki/Glucoside" title="Glucoside">glucosides</a> in six peach and six nectarine cultivars<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Malvin" title="Malvin">malvin</a> glycosides in clingstone peaches.<sup id="cite_ref-Chang_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chang-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
As with many other members of the <a href="/wiki/Rose_family" class="mw-redirect" title="Rose family">rose family</a>, peach seeds contain <a href="/wiki/Glycoside#Cyanogenic_glycosides" title="Glycoside">cyanogenic glycosides</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Amygdalin" title="Amygdalin">amygdalin</a> (note the <a href="/wiki/Subgenus" title="Subgenus">subgenus</a> designation: <i>Amygdalus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Lee_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lee-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> These substances are capable of decomposing into a sugar molecule and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cyanide" title="Hydrogen cyanide">hydrogen cyanide</a> gas.<sup id="cite_ref-cho_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cho-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lee_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lee-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Cyanogenic glycosides are toxic if consumed in large doses.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> While peach seeds are not the most toxic within the rose family (see <a href="/wiki/Bitter_almond" class="mw-redirect" title="Bitter almond">bitter almond</a>), large consumption of these chemicals from any source is potentially hazardous to animal and human health.<sup id="cite_ref-cho_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cho-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Peach <a href="/wiki/Food_allergy" title="Food allergy">allergy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Food_intolerance" title="Food intolerance">intolerance</a> is a relatively common form of hypersensitivity to <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> contained in peaches and related fruits (such as <a href="/wiki/Almond" title="Almond">almonds</a>). Symptoms range from local effects (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Oral_allergy_syndrome" title="Oral allergy syndrome">oral allergy syndrome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Contact_urticaria" class="mw-redirect" title="Contact urticaria">contact urticaria</a>) to more severe systemic reactions, including <a href="/wiki/Anaphylaxis" title="Anaphylaxis">anaphylaxis</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Urticaria" class="mw-redirect" title="Urticaria">urticaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angioedema" title="Angioedema">angioedema</a>, gastrointestinal and respiratory symptoms).<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Adverse reactions are related to the "freshness" of the fruit: peeled or canned fruit may be tolerated.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aroma">Aroma</span></h3>
<p>Some 110 chemical compounds contribute to peach aroma, including <a href="/wiki/Alcohols" class="mw-redirect" title="Alcohols">alcohols</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ketones" class="mw-redirect" title="Ketones">ketones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aldehydes" class="mw-redirect" title="Aldehydes">aldehydes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Esters" class="mw-redirect" title="Esters">esters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polyphenols" class="mw-redirect" title="Polyphenols">polyphenols</a> and <a href="/wiki/Terpenoids" class="mw-redirect" title="Terpenoids">terpenoids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
